<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Skeletal muscle</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">One of three major  skeletal  system  types that connect to bones</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Muscle" redirects here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Muscle_(disambiguation)" title="Muscle (disambiguation)">Muscle (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-below{text-align:center}</style><table class="infobox"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-above" colspan="2" class="infobox-below" colspan="2"><a href="/wiki/Anatomical_terminology" title="Anatomical terminology">Anatomical terminology</a><div style="text-align: right;"><small class="noprint">[<a class="extiw" href="https://www.wikidata.org/wiki/Q1048687" title="d:Q1048687">edit on Wikidata</a>]</small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Skeletal muscles</b> (commonly referred to as <b>muscles</b>) are <a href="/wiki/Organ_(biology)" title="Organ (biology)">organs</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrate</a> <a href="/wiki/Muscular_system" title="Muscular system">muscular system</a> and typically are attached by <a href="/wiki/Tendon" title="Tendon">tendons</a> to <a href="/wiki/Bone" title="Bone">bones</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Skeleton" title="Skeleton">skeleton</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-opentext_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-opentext-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SEER_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-SEER-2">[2]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Muscle_cell" title="Muscle cell">muscle cells</a> of skeletal muscles are much longer than in the other types of <a href="/wiki/Muscle_tissue" title="Muscle tissue">muscle tissue</a>, and are often known as <a href="/wiki/Skeletal_muscle#Skeletal_muscle_fibers" title="Skeletal muscle">muscle fibers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore1_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moore1-3">[3]</a></sup> The muscle tissue of a skeletal muscle is <a href="/wiki/Striated_muscle_tissue" title="Striated muscle tissue">striated</a> – having a striped appearance due to the arrangement of the <a href="/wiki/Sarcomere" title="Sarcomere">sarcomeres</a>.
</p><p>Skeletal muscles are voluntary muscles under the control of the <a href="/wiki/Somatic_nervous_system" title="Somatic nervous system">somatic nervous system</a>. The other types of muscle are <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_muscle" title="Cardiac muscle">cardiac muscle</a> which is also striated and <a href="/wiki/Smooth_muscle" title="Smooth muscle">smooth muscle</a> which is non-striated; both of these types of muscle tissue are classified as involuntary, or, under the control of the <a href="/wiki/Autonomic_nervous_system" title="Autonomic nervous system">autonomic nervous system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>A skeletal muscle contains multiple <a href="/wiki/Muscle_fascicle" title="Muscle fascicle">fascicles</a> – bundles of muscle fibers.  Each individual fiber, and each muscle is surrounded by a type of <a href="/wiki/Connective_tissue" title="Connective tissue">connective tissue</a> layer of <a href="/wiki/Fascia" title="Fascia">fascia</a>. Muscle fibers are formed from the <a href="/wiki/Cell_fusion" title="Cell fusion">fusion</a> of developmental <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Myoblasts" title="Myoblasts">myoblasts</a> in a process known as <a href="/wiki/Myogenesis" title="Myogenesis">myogenesis</a> resulting in long <a href="/wiki/Multinucleate" title="Multinucleate">multinucleated</a> cells. In these cells the <a href="/wiki/Cell_nucleus" title="Cell nucleus">nuclei</a> termed <i>myonuclei</i> are located along the inside of the <a href="/wiki/Sarcolemma" title="Sarcolemma">cell membrane</a>. Muscle fibers also have multiple <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mitochondria" title="Mitochondria">mitochondria</a> to meet energy needs.
</p><p>Muscle fibers are in turn composed of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Myofibrils" title="Myofibrils">myofibrils</a>. The myofibrils are composed of <a href="/wiki/Actin" title="Actin">actin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Myosin" title="Myosin">myosin</a> filaments called <a href="/wiki/Myofilament" title="Myofilament">myofilaments</a>, repeated in units called sarcomeres, which are the basic functional, contractile units of the muscle fiber necessary for <a href="/wiki/Muscle_contraction" title="Muscle contraction">muscle contraction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Henderson_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Henderson-5">[5]</a></sup> Muscles are predominantly powered by the <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">oxidation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fats</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a>, but <a href="/wiki/Lactic_acid_fermentation" title="Lactic acid fermentation">anaerobic</a> chemical reactions are also used, particularly by <a href="/wiki/Skeletal_muscle#Twitch_speed" title="Skeletal muscle">fast twitch fibers</a>. These chemical reactions produce <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">adenosine triphosphate</a> (ATP) molecules that are used to power the movement of the <a href="/wiki/Myosin_head" title="Myosin head">myosin heads</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gross_anatomy">Gross anatomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skeletal_muscle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Gross anatomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_skeletal_muscles_of_the_human_body" title="List of skeletal muscles of the human body">List of skeletal muscles of the human body</a></div>

style="width:642px;max-width:642px"><div class="trow"><div class="theader" style="text-align:center">Front and back views of the major skeletal muscles of the human body</div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:320px;max-width:320px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:369px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Muscles_anterior_labeled.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="1342" data-file-width="1156" decoding="async" height="369" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Muscles_anterior_labeled.png/318px-Muscles_anterior_labeled.png" muscles</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:318px;max-width:318px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:369px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Muscle_posterior_labeled.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="1297" data-file-width="1063" decoding="async" height="386" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Muscle_posterior_labeled.png/316px-Muscle_posterior_labeled.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Muscle_posterior_labeled.png/474px-Muscle_posterior_labeled.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Muscle_posterior_labeled.png/632px-Muscle_posterior_labeled.png 2x" width="316"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Back view of major skeletal muscles</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>There are more than 600 skeletal muscles in the human body, making up around 40% to 50% of body weight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SEER1_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-SEER1-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LoC_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-LoC-8">[8]</a></sup> Most muscles occur in bilaterally-placed pairs to serve both sides of the body. Muscles are often classed as <b>groups of muscles</b> that work together to carry out an action. In the <a href="/wiki/Torso" title="Torso">torso</a> there are several <a href="/wiki/Torso#Major_muscle_groups" title="Torso">major muscle groups</a> including the <a href="/wiki/Pectoral_muscles" title="Pectoral muscles">pectoral</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Abdomen#Muscles" title="Abdomen">abdominal muscles</a>; <a href="/wiki/Anatomical_terms_of_muscle#Intrinsic_and_extrinsic_muscles" title="Anatomical terms of muscle">intrinsic and extrinsic muscles</a> are subdivisions of muscle groups in the <a href="/wiki/Hand#Muscles" title="Hand">hand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Foot#Muscles" title="Foot">foot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tongue#Muscles" title="Tongue">tongue</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Extraocular_muscles" title="Extraocular muscles">extraocular muscles</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Human_eye" title="Human eye">eye</a>. Muscles are also grouped into <a href="/wiki/Fascial_compartment" title="Fascial compartment">compartments</a> including <a href="/wiki/Fascial_compartments_of_arm" title="Fascial compartments of arm">four groups in the arm</a>,
and the <a href="/wiki/Fascial_compartments_of_leg" title="Fascial compartments of leg">four groups in the leg</a>.
</p><p>Apart from the contractile part of a muscle consisting of its fibers, a muscle contains a non-contractile part of dense fibrous connective tissue that makes up the <a href="/wiki/Tendon" title="Tendon">tendon</a> at each end. The tendons attach the muscles to bones to give skeletal movement. The length of a muscle includes the tendons. <a href="/wiki/Connective_tissue" title="Connective tissue">Connective tissue</a> is present in all muscles as <a href="/wiki/Deep_fascia" title="Deep fascia">deep fascia</a>. Deep fascia specialises within muscles to enclose each muscle fiber as <a href="/wiki/Endomysium" title="Endomysium">endomysium</a>; each muscle fascicle as <a href="/wiki/Perimysium" title="Perimysium">perimysium</a>, and each individual muscle as <a href="/wiki/Epimysium" title="Epimysium">epimysium</a>. Together these layers are called <i>mysia</i>. Deep fascia also separates the groups of muscles into muscle compartments.
</p><p>Two types of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sensory_receptor" title="Sensory receptor">sensory receptors</a> found in muscles are <a href="/wiki/Muscle_spindle" title="Muscle spindle">muscle spindles</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Golgi_tendon_organ" title="Golgi tendon organ">Golgi tendon organs</a>. Muscle spindles are <a href="/wiki/Stretch_receptor" title="Stretch receptor">stretch receptors</a> located in the muscle belly. Golgi tendon organs are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proprioceptor" title="Proprioceptor">proprioceptors</a> located at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Myotendinous_junction" title="Myotendinous junction">myotendinous junction</a> that inform of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muscle_tension" title="Muscle tension">muscle's tension</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Skeletal_muscle_fibers">Skeletal muscle fibers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skeletal_muscle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Skeletal muscle fibers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blausen_0801_SkeletalMuscle.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1823" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Blausen_0801_SkeletalMuscle.png/220px-Blausen_0801_SkeletalMuscle.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Blausen_0801_SkeletalMuscle.png/330px-Blausen_0801_SkeletalMuscle.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Blausen_0801_SkeletalMuscle.png/440px-Blausen_0801_SkeletalMuscle.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Blausen_0801_SkeletalMuscle.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>3D rendering of a skeletal muscle fiber</div></div></div>
<p>Skeletal muscle cells are the individual contractile cells within a muscle, and are often termed as muscle fibers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SEER_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-SEER-2">[2]</a></sup> A single muscle such as the <a href="/wiki/Biceps" title="Biceps">biceps</a> in a young adult male contains around 253,000 muscle fibers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Klein_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Klein-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>Skeletal muscle fibers are the only muscle cells that are <a href="/wiki/Multinucleate" title="Multinucleate">multinucleated</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Cell_nucleus" title="Cell nucleus">nuclei</a> often referred to as <b>myonuclei</b>. This occurs during <a href="/wiki/Myogenesis" title="Myogenesis">myogenesis</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Cell_fusion" title="Cell fusion">fusion</a> of myoblasts each contributing a nucleus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cho_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cho-10">[10]</a></sup> Fusion depends on muscle-specific proteins known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fusogen" title="Fusogen">fusogens</a> called <i>myomaker</i> and <i>myomerger</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prasad_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Prasad-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many nuclei are needed by the skeletal muscle cell for the large amounts of proteins and enzymes needed to be produced for the cell's normal functioning.   
A single muscle fiber can contain from hundreds to thousands of nuclei.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Acta_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Acta-12">[12]</a></sup> A muscle fiber for example in the human biceps with a length of 10 cm can have as many as 3000 nuclei.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Acta_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Acta-12">[12]</a></sup> Unlike in a non-muscle <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cell</a> where the nucleus is centrally positioned, the myonucleus is elongated and located close to the <a href="/wiki/Sarcolemma" title="Sarcolemma">sarcolemma</a>. The myonuclei are quite uniformly arranged along the fiber with each nucleus having its own <i>myonuclear domain</i> where it is responsible for supporting the volume of cytoplasm in that particular section of the myofiber.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prasad_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Prasad-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Acta_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-Acta-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>A group of muscle <a href="/wiki/Stem_cell" title="Stem cell">stem cells</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Myosatellite_cell" title="Myosatellite cell">myosatellite cells</a>, also <i>satellite cells</i> are found between the <a href="/wiki/Basement_membrane" title="Basement membrane">basement membrane</a> and the sarcolemma of muscle fibers. These cells are normally quiescent but can be activated by exercise or pathology to provide additional myonuclei for muscle growth or repair.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Quarta_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Quarta-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Attachment_to_tendons">Attachment to tendons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skeletal_muscle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Attachment to tendons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Muscles attach to <a href="/wiki/Tendon" title="Tendon">tendons</a> in a complex interface region known as the <b>musculotendinous junction</b> also known as the <b>myotendinous junction</b>, an area specialised for the primary transmission of force.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Charvet_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Charvet-14">[14]</a></sup> At the muscle-tendon interface, force is transmitted from the sarcomeres in the muscle cells to the tendon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Henderson_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Henderson-5">[5]</a></sup> Muscles and tendons develop in close association, and after their joining at the myotendinous junction they constitute a dynamic unit for the transmission of force from muscle contraction to the skeletal system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Charvet_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Charvet-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Arrangement_of_muscle_fibers">Arrangement of muscle fibers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skeletal_muscle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Arrangement of muscle fibers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Muscle_architecture" title="Muscle architecture">Muscle architecture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Muscle_Types.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2500" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Muscle_Types.png/220px-Muscle_Types.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Muscle_Types.png/330px-Muscle_Types.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Muscle_Types.png/440px-Muscle_Types.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Muscle_Types.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Muscle types by fiber arrangement</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fiederung.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="343" data-file-width="464" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Fiederung.svg/220px-Fiederung.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Fiederung.svg/330px-Fiederung.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Fiederung.svg/440px-Fiederung.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fiederung.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Types of <a href="/wiki/Pennate_muscle" title="Pennate muscle">pennate muscle</a>. A – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unipennate" title="Unipennate">unipennate</a>;  B – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bipennate" title="Bipennate">bipennate</a>;  C – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multipennate" title="Multipennate">multipennate</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Muscle_architecture" title="Muscle architecture">Muscle architecture</a> refers to the arrangement of muscle fibers relative to the axis of <a href="/wiki/Muscle_architecture#Force_generation" title="Muscle architecture">force generation</a>, which runs from a <a href="/wiki/Anatomical_terms_of_muscle#Origin" title="Anatomical terms of muscle">muscle's origin</a> to its <a href="/wiki/Anatomical_terms_of_muscle#Insertion" title="Anatomical terms of muscle">insertion</a>. 
The usual arrangements are types of <a href="/wiki/Muscle_architecture#Parallel" title="Muscle architecture">parallel</a>, and types of <a href="/wiki/Pennate_muscle" title="Pennate muscle">pennate muscle</a>. In parallel muscles the fascicles run parallel to the axis of force generation, but the fascicles can vary in their relationship to one another, and to their tendons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Martini_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Martini-15">[15]</a></sup> These variations are seen in <a href="/wiki/Muscle_architecture#Fusiform" title="Muscle architecture">fusiform</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muscle_architecture#Strap" title="Muscle architecture">strap</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Muscle_architecture#Convergent" title="Muscle architecture">convergent muscles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore1_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Moore1-3">[3]</a></sup> A convergent muscle has a triangular or fan-shape as the fibers converge at its insertion and are fanned out broadly at the origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Martini_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Martini-15">[15]</a></sup> A less common example of a parallel muscle is a circular muscle such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orbicularis_oculi" title="Orbicularis oculi">orbicularis oculi</a>, in which the fibers are longitudinally arranged, but create a circle from origin to insertion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lieber,_Richard_L_2002_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lieber,_Richard_L_2002-16">[16]</a></sup> These different architectures, can cause variations in the tension that a muscle can create between its tendons.
</p><p>The fibers in <a href="/wiki/Pennate_muscle" title="Pennate muscle">pennate muscles</a> run at an angle to the axis of force generation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lieber,_Richard_L_2002_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lieber,_Richard_L_2002-16">[16]</a></sup> This <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pennation_angle" title="Pennation angle">pennation angle</a> reduces the effective force of any individual fiber, as it is effectively pulling off-axis.  However, because of this angle, more fibers can be packed into the same muscle volume, increasing the <a href="/wiki/Physiological_cross-sectional_area" title="Physiological cross-sectional area">physiological cross-sectional area</a> (PCSA).  This effect is known as fiber packing, and in terms of force generation, it more than overcomes the efficiency-loss of the off-axis orientation. The trade-off comes in overall speed of muscle shortening and in the total excursion.  Overall muscle shortening speed is reduced compared to fiber shortening speed, as is the total distance of shortening.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lieber,_Richard_L_2002_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lieber,_Richard_L_2002-16">[16]</a></sup>  All of these effects scale with pennation angle; greater angles lead to greater force due to increased fiber packing and PCSA, but with greater losses in shortening speed and excursion. Types of pennate muscle are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unipennate" title="Unipennate">unipennate</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bipennate" title="Bipennate">bipennate</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multipennate" title="Multipennate">multipennate</a>. A unipennate muscle has similarly angled fibers that are on one side of a tendon. A bipennate muscle has fibers on two sides of a tendon. Multipennate muscles have fibers that are oriented at multiple angles along the force-generating axis, and this is the most general and most common architecture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lieber,_Richard_L_2002_16-3"><a href="#cite_note-Lieber,_Richard_L_2002-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Muscle_fiber_growth">Muscle fiber growth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skeletal_muscle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Muscle fiber growth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Muscle fibers grow when exercised and shrink when not in use. This is due to the fact that exercise stimulates the increase in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Myofibrils" title="Myofibrils">myofibrils</a> which increase the overall size of muscle cells. Well exercised muscles can not only add more size but can also develop more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mitochondria" title="Mitochondria">mitochondria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Myoglobin" title="Myoglobin">myoglobin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glycogen" title="Glycogen">glycogen</a> and a higher density of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Capillaries" title="Capillaries">capillaries</a>. However muscle cells cannot divide to produce new cells, and as a result there are fewer muscle cells in  an adult than in a newborn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ziser_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-ziser-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Muscle_naming">Muscle naming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skeletal_muscle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Muscle naming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Anatomical_terms_of_motion" title="Anatomical terms of motion">Anatomical terms of motion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anatomical_terms_of_location" title="Anatomical terms of location">Anatomical terms of location</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Anatomical_terms_of_muscle" title="Anatomical terms of muscle">Anatomical terms of muscle</a></div>
<p>There are a number of terms used in the naming of muscles including those relating to size, shape, action, location, their orientation, and their number of heads.
</p>
<dl><dt>By size</dt>
<dd><i>brevis</i> means short; <i>longus</i> means long; <i>longissimus</i> means longest; <i>magnus</i> means large; <i>major</i> means larger; <i>maximus</i> means largest; <i>minor</i> means smaller, and <i>minimus</i> smallest; <i>latissimus</i> means widest, and <i>vastus</i> means huge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TortDerr_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-TortDerr-18">[18]</a></sup> These terms are often used after the particular muscle such as <a href="/wiki/Gluteus_maximus" title="Gluteus maximus">gluteus maximus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gluteus_minimus" title="Gluteus minimus">gluteus minimus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saladin2_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Saladin2-19">[19]</a></sup></dd>
<dt>By relative shape</dt>
<dd><i>deltoid</i> means triangular; <i>quadratus</i> means having four sides; <i>rhomboideus</i> means having a <a href="/wiki/Rhomboid" title="Rhomboid">rhomboid</a> shape; <i>teres</i> means round or cylindrical, and <i>trapezius</i> means having a <a href="/wiki/Trapezoid" title="Trapezoid">trapezoid</a> shape;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saladin2_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Saladin2-19">[19]</a></sup> <i>serratus</i> means saw-toothed; <i>orbicularis</i> means circular; <i>pectinate</i> means comblike; <i>piriformis</i> means pear-shaped; <i>platys</i> means flat and <i>gracilis</i> means slender.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TortDerr_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-TortDerr-18">[18]</a></sup> Examples are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pronator_teres" title="Pronator teres">pronator teres</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pronator_quadratus" title="Pronator quadratus">pronator quadratus</a>.</dd>
<dt>By action</dt>
<dd><i><a href="/wiki/Anatomical_terms_of_motion#Abduction_and_adduction" title="Anatomical terms of motion">abductor</a></i> moving away from the midline; <i><a href="/wiki/Anatomical_terms_of_motion#Abduction_and_adduction" title="Anatomical terms of motion">adductor</a></i> moving towards the midline; <i><a href="/wiki/List_of_depressors_of_the_human_body" title="List of depressors of the human body">depressor</a></i> moving downwards; <i><a href="/wiki/List_of_elevators_of_the_human_body" title="List of elevators of the human body">elevator</a></i> moving upwards; <i><a href="/wiki/List_of_flexors_of_the_human_body" title="List of flexors of the human body">flexor</a></i> moving that decreases an angle; <i><a href="/wiki/List_of_extensors_of_the_human_body" title="List of extensors of the human body">extensor</a></i> moving that increase an angle or straightens; <i><a href="/wiki/Anatomical_terms_of_motion#Pronation_and_supination" title="Anatomical terms of motion">pronator</a></i> moving <a href="/wiki/Prone_position" title="Prone position">to face down</a>; <i><a href="/wiki/Anatomical_terms_of_motion#Pronation_and_supination" title="Anatomical terms of motion">supinator</a></i> moving <a href="/wiki/Supine_position" title="Supine position">to face upwards</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saladin2_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-Saladin2-19">[19]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/List_of_internal_rotators_of_the_human_body" title="List of internal rotators of the human body">internal rotator</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Anatomical_terms_of_motion#Rotation" title="Anatomical terms of motion">rotating</a> towards the body; <i><a href="/wiki/List_of_external_rotators_of_the_human_body" title="List of external rotators of the human body">external rotator</a></i> rotating away from the body; <i><a href="/wiki/Sphincter" title="Sphincter">sphincter</a></i> decreases the size, and <i>tensor</i> gives tension to; <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fixator_muscle" title="Fixator muscle">fixator muscles</a></i> serve to fix a joint in a given position by stabilizing the prime mover whilst other joints are moving.</dd>
<dt>By number of heads</dt>
<dd><i>biceps</i> two; <i>triceps</i> three and <i>quadriceps</i> four.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saladin2_19-3"><a href="#cite_note-Saladin2-19">[19]</a></sup></dd>
<dt>By location</dt>
<dd>named after the near main structure such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temporal_muscle" title="Temporal muscle">temporal muscle</a> (temporalis) near to the <a href="/wiki/Temporal_bone" title="Temporal bone">temporal bone</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TortDerr_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-TortDerr-18">[18]</a></sup> Also <i>supra-</i> above; <i>infra-</i> below, and <i>sub-</i> under.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SEER1_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-SEER1-7">[7]</a></sup></dd>
<dt>By fascicle orientation</dt>
<dd>Relative to the midline, <i>rectus</i> means parallel to the midline; <i>transverse</i> means perpendicular to the midline, and <i>oblique</i> means diagonal to the midline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TortDerr_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-TortDerr-18">[18]</a></sup> Relative to the axis of the generation of force – types of <i>parallel</i>, and types of <i>pennate</i> muscles.</dd></dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fiber_types">Fiber types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skeletal_muscle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Fiber types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Broadly there are two types of muscle fiber: <b>Type I</b>, which is slow, and <b>Type II</b> which are fast. Type II has two divisions of type IIA (oxidative), and type IIX (glycolytic), giving three main fiber types.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-opentext1_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-opentext1-20">[20]</a></sup> These fibers have relatively distinct metabolic, contractile, and <a href="/wiki/Motor_unit" title="Motor unit">motor unit</a> properties. The table below differentiates these types of properties. These types of properties—while they are partly dependent on the properties of individual fibers—tend to be relevant and measured at the level of the motor unit, rather than individual fiber.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacIntoshGardiner2006_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-MacIntoshGardiner2006-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="margin: 1em auto 1em auto;">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fiber_color">Fiber color</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skeletal_muscle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Fiber color">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Traditionally, fibers were categorized depending on their varying color, which is a reflection of <a href="/wiki/Myoglobin" title="Myoglobin">myoglobin</a> content. Type I fibers appear red due to the high levels of myoglobin. Red muscle fibers tend to have more mitochondria and greater local capillary density. These fibers are more suited for endurance and are slow to fatigue because they use <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidative_metabolism" title="Oxidative metabolism">oxidative metabolism</a> to generate ATP (<a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">adenosine triphosphate</a>). Less oxidative Type II fibers are white due to relatively low myoglobin and a reliance on glycolytic enzymes.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Twitch_speed">Twitch speed</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skeletal_muscle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Twitch speed">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Fibers can also be classified on their twitch capabilities, into fast and slow twitch. These traits largely, but not completely, overlap the classifications based on color, ATPase, or MHC.
</p><p>Some authors define a fast twitch fiber as one in which the myosin can split ATP very quickly.  These mainly include the ATPase type II and MHC type II fibers.  However, fast twitch fibers also demonstrate a higher capability for electrochemical transmission of action potentials and a rapid level of calcium release and uptake by the sarcoplasmic reticulum. The fast twitch fibers rely on a well-developed, <a href="/wiki/Bioenergetic_systems#Aerobic_and_anaerobic_metabolism" title="Bioenergetic systems">anaerobic</a>, short term, glycolytic system for energy transfer and can contract and develop tension at 2–3 times the rate of slow twitch fibers. Fast twitch muscles are much better at generating short bursts of strength or speed than slow muscles, and so fatigue more quickly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-about.com_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-about.com-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>The slow twitch fibers generate energy for ATP re-synthesis by means of a long term system of <a href="/wiki/Bioenergetic_systems#Aerobic_and_anaerobic_metabolism" title="Bioenergetic systems">aerobic</a> energy transfer. These mainly include the ATPase type I and MHC type I fibers. They tend to have a low activity level of ATPase, a slower speed of contraction with a less well developed glycolytic capacity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-about.com_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-about.com-22">[22]</a></sup> Fibers that become slow-twitch develop greater numbers of  mitochondria and capillaries making them better for prolonged work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacIntosh1_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-MacIntosh1-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dt>Type distribution</dt>
<dd></dd></dl>
<p>Individual muscles tend to be a mixture of various fiber types, but their proportions vary depending on the actions of that muscle. For instance, in humans, the <a href="/wiki/Quadriceps" title="Quadriceps">quadriceps</a> muscles contain ~52% type I fibers, while the <a href="/wiki/Soleus_muscle" title="Soleus muscle">soleus</a> is ~80% type I.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnson,_M_A_1973_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson,_M_A_1973-24">[24]</a></sup> The orbicularis oculi muscle of the eye is only ~15% type I.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnson,_M_A_1973_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson,_M_A_1973-24">[24]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Motor_units" title="Motor units">Motor units</a> within the muscle, however, have minimal variation between the fibers of that unit.  It is this fact that makes the size principal of <a href="/wiki/Motor_unit_recruitment" title="Motor unit recruitment">motor unit recruitment</a> viable.
</p><p>The total number of skeletal muscle fibers has traditionally been thought not to change.
It is believed there are no sex or age differences in fiber distribution; however, proportions of fiber types vary considerably from muscle to muscle and person to person.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Among different species there is much variation in the proportions of muscle fiber types.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schiaffino_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schiaffino-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sedentary men and women (as well as young children) have 45% type II and 55% type I fibers.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
People at the higher end of any sport tend to demonstrate patterns of fiber distribution e.g. endurance athletes show a higher level of type I fibers.
Sprint athletes, on the other hand, require large numbers of type IIX fibers.
Middle-distance event athletes show approximately equal distribution of the two types. This is also often the case for power athletes such as throwers and jumpers.
It has been suggested that various types of exercise can induce changes in the fibers of a skeletal muscle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>It is thought that if you perform endurance type events for a sustained period of time, some of the type IIX fibers transform into type IIA fibers. However, there is no consensus on the subject.
It may well be that the type IIX fibers show enhancements of the oxidative capacity after high intensity endurance training which brings them to a level at which they are able to perform oxidative metabolism as effectively as slow twitch fibers of untrained subjects. This would be brought about by an increase in mitochondrial size and number and the associated related changes, not a change in fiber type.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fiber_typing_methods">Fiber typing methods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skeletal_muscle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Fiber typing methods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Denervation_atrophy_-_atp94_-_intermed_mag.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4272" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Denervation_atrophy_-_atp94_-_intermed_mag.jpg/220px-Denervation_atrophy_-_atp94_-_intermed_mag.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Denervation_atrophy_-_atp94_-_intermed_mag.jpg/330px-Denervation_atrophy_-_atp94_-_intermed_mag.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Denervation_atrophy_-_atp94_-_intermed_mag.jpg/440px-Denervation_atrophy_-_atp94_-_intermed_mag.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Denervation_atrophy_-_atp94_-_intermed_mag.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>ATPase staining of a muscle cross section.  Type II fibers are dark, due to the alkaline pH of the preparation. In this example, the size of the type II fibers is considerably less than the type I fibers due to denervation atrophy.</div></div></div>
<p>There are numerous methods employed for fiber-typing, and confusion between the methods is common among non-experts. Two commonly confused methods are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Histochemical" title="Histochemical">histochemical</a> staining for <a href="/wiki/Myosin_ATPase" title="Myosin ATPase">myosin ATPase</a> activity and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immunohistochemical" title="Immunohistochemical">immunohistochemical</a> staining for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Myosin_heavy_chain" title="Myosin heavy chain">myosin heavy chain</a> (MHC) type.  Myosin ATPase activity is commonly—and correctly—referred to as simply "fiber type", and results from the direct assaying of ATPase activity under various conditions (e.g. <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacIntoshGardiner2006_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-MacIntoshGardiner2006-21">[21]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Myosin_heavy_chain" title="Myosin heavy chain">Myosin heavy chain</a> staining is most accurately referred to as "MHC fiber type", e.g. "MHC IIa fibers", and results from determination of different MHC <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isoforms" title="Isoforms">isoforms</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacIntoshGardiner2006_21-3"><a href="#cite_note-MacIntoshGardiner2006-21">[21]</a></sup> These methods are closely related physiologically, as the MHC type is the primary determinant of ATPase activity. However, neither of these typing methods is directly metabolic in nature; they do not directly address oxidative or glycolytic capacity of the fiber.
</p><p>When "type I" or "type II" fibers are referred to generically, this most accurately refers to the sum of numerical fiber types (I vs. II) as assessed by myosin ATPase activity staining (e.g. "type II" fibers refers to type IIA + type IIAX + type IIXA ... etc.).
</p><p>Below is a table showing the relationship between these two methods, limited to fiber types found in humans. Subtype capitalization is used in fiber typing vs. MHC typing, and some ATPase types actually contain multiple MHC types.  Also, a <b>subtype B or b is not expressed in humans by either method</b>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid7545970_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid7545970-27">[27]</a></sup> Early researchers believed humans to express a MHC IIb, which led to the ATPase classification of IIB. However, later research showed that the human MHC IIb was in fact IIx,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid7545970_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid7545970-27">[27]</a></sup> indicating that the IIB is better named IIX. IIb is expressed in other mammals, so is still accurately seen (along with IIB) in the literature.  Non human fiber types include true IIb fibers, IIc, IId, etc.
</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="margin: 1em auto 1em auto;">
<caption><b>ATPase Vs. MHC fiber types<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacIntoshGardiner2006_21-4"><a href="#cite_note-MacIntoshGardiner2006-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid10998639_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid10998639-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></b>
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th scope="col" width="90">ATPase type
</th>
<th scope="col" width="140">MHC heavy chain(s)
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td>Type I</td>
<td>MHC Iβ
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Type IC</td>
<td>MHC Iβ &gt; MHC IIa
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Type IIC</td>
<td>MHC IIa &gt; MHC Iβ
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Type IIA</td>
<td>MHC IIa
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Type IIAX</td>
<td>MHC IIa &gt; MHC IIx
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Type IIXA</td>
<td>MHC IIx &gt; MHC IIa
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Type IIX</td>
<td>MHC IIx
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><span class="anchor" id="type_I_anchor"></span>
Further fiber typing methods are less formally delineated, and exist on more of a spectrum. They tend to be focused more on metabolic and functional capacities (i.e., oxidative vs. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glycolytic" title="Glycolytic">glycolytic</a>, fast vs. slow contraction time). As noted above, fiber typing by ATPase or MHC does not directly measure or dictate these parameters. However, many of the various methods are mechanistically linked, while others are correlated <i>in vivo</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid5472111_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid5472111-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid430414_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid430414-31">[31]</a></sup> For instance, ATPase fiber type is related to contraction speed, because high ATPase activity allows faster <a href="/wiki/Muscle_contraction#Crossbridge_cycling" title="Muscle contraction">crossbridge cycling</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacIntoshGardiner2006_21-5"><a href="#cite_note-MacIntoshGardiner2006-21">[21]</a></sup> While ATPase activity is only one component of contraction speed, Type I fibers are "slow", in part, because they have low speeds of ATPase activity in comparison to Type II fibers. However, measuring contraction speed is not the same as ATPase fiber typing.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Microanatomy">Microanatomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skeletal_muscle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Microanatomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sarcomere.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="251" data-file-width="374" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Sarcomere.gif/220px-Sarcomere.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Sarcomere.gif/330px-Sarcomere.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a4/Sarcomere.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sarcomere.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Structure of muscle fibre showing a sarcomere under <a href="/wiki/Electron_microscope" title="Electron microscope">electron microscope</a> with schematic explanation.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1023_T-tubule.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="368" data-file-width="577" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/1023_T-tubule.jpg/220px-1023_T-tubule.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/1023_T-tubule.jpg/330px-1023_T-tubule.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/1023_T-tubule.jpg/440px-1023_T-tubule.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1023_T-tubule.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diagram of sarcoplasmic reticulum with terminal cisternae and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T-tubules" title="T-tubules">T-tubules</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Skeletal muscle exhibits a distinctive banding pattern when viewed under the microscope due to the arrangement of two contractile proteins <a href="/wiki/Myosin" title="Myosin">myosin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Actin" title="Actin">actin</a> – that are two of the <a href="/wiki/Myofilament" title="Myofilament">myofilaments</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Myofibril" title="Myofibril">myofibrils</a>. The myosin forms the thick filaments, and actin forms the thin filaments, and are arranged in repeating units called <a href="/wiki/Sarcomere" title="Sarcomere">sarcomeres</a>. The interaction of both proteins results in muscle contraction.
</p><p>The sarcomere is attached to other organelles such as the mitochondria by <a href="/wiki/Intermediate_filament" title="Intermediate filament">intermediate filaments</a> in the cytoskeleton. The <a href="/wiki/Costamere" title="Costamere">costamere</a> attaches the sarcomere to the sarcolemma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Henderson_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-Henderson-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>Every single organelle and macromolecule of a muscle fiber is arranged to ensure that it meets desired functions. The <a href="/wiki/Cell_membrane" title="Cell membrane">cell membrane</a> is called the sarcolemma with the cytoplasm known as the <a href="/wiki/Sarcoplasm" title="Sarcoplasm">sarcoplasm</a>. In the sarcoplasm are the myofibrils. The myofibrils are long protein bundles about one micrometer in diameter. Pressed against the inside of the sarcolemma are the unusual flattened myonuclei. Between the myofibrils are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mitochondria" title="Mitochondria">mitochondria</a>.
</p><p>While the muscle fiber does not have smooth endoplasmic cisternae, it contains <a href="/wiki/Sarcoplasmic_reticulum" title="Sarcoplasmic reticulum">sarcoplasmic reticulum</a>. The sarcoplasmic reticulum surrounds the myofibrils and holds a reserve of the <a href="/wiki/Calcium_in_biology" title="Calcium in biology">calcium ions</a> needed to cause a muscle contraction. Periodically, it has dilated end sacs known as <a href="/wiki/Terminal_cisternae" title="Terminal cisternae">terminal cisternae</a>. These cross the muscle fiber from one side to the other. In between two terminal cisternae is a tubular infolding called a transverse tubule (T tubule). <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T_tubule" title="T tubule">T tubules</a> are the pathways for action potentials to signal the sarcoplasmic reticulum to release calcium, causing a muscle contraction. Together, two terminal cisternae and a transverse tubule form a <a href="/wiki/Triad_(anatomy)" title="Triad (anatomy)">triad</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Development">Development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skeletal_muscle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Myogenesis" title="Myogenesis">Myogenesis</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gray20.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="450" data-file-width="304" decoding="async" height="266" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Gray20.png/180px-Gray20.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Gray20.png/270px-Gray20.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c0/Gray20.png 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gray20.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_embryo" title="Human embryo">Human embryo</a> showing <a href="/wiki/Somite" title="Somite">somites</a> labelled as <i>primitive segments</i>.</div></div></div>
<p>All muscles are derived from <a href="/wiki/Paraxial_mesoderm" title="Paraxial mesoderm">paraxial mesoderm</a>. During <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Embryonic_development" title="Embryonic development">embryonic development</a> in the process of <a href="/wiki/Somitogenesis" title="Somitogenesis">somitogenesis</a> the paraxial mesoderm is divided along the <a href="/wiki/Embryo" title="Embryo">embryo</a>'s length to form <a href="/wiki/Somite" title="Somite">somites</a>, corresponding to the <a href="/wiki/Segmentation_(biology)" title="Segmentation (biology)">segmentation</a> of the body most obviously seen in the <a href="/wiki/Vertebral_column" title="Vertebral column">vertebral column</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sweeney_1997_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sweeney_1997-33">[33]</a></sup> Each somite has three divisions, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sclerotome" title="Sclerotome">sclerotome</a> (which forms <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vertebra_(anatomy)" title="Vertebra (anatomy)">vertebrae</a>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dermatome_(embryology)" title="Dermatome (embryology)">dermatome</a> (which forms skin), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Myotome_(embryology)" title="Myotome (embryology)">myotome</a> (which forms muscle). The myotome is divided into two sections, the epimere and hypomere, which form <a href="/wiki/Epaxial_and_hypaxial_muscles" title="Epaxial and hypaxial muscles">epaxial and hypaxial muscles</a>, respectively. The only epaxial muscles in humans are the <a href="/wiki/Erector_spinae_muscles" title="Erector spinae muscles">erector spinae</a> and small vertebral muscles, and are innervated by the dorsal rami of the <a href="/wiki/Spinal_nerve" title="Spinal nerve">spinal nerves</a>. All other muscles, including those of the limbs are hypaxial, and innervated by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ventral_rami" title="Ventral rami">ventral rami</a> of the spinal nerves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sweeney_1997_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sweeney_1997-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>During development, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Myoblast" title="Myoblast">myoblasts</a> (muscle progenitor cells) either remain in the somite to form muscles associated with the vertebral column or migrate out into the body to form all other muscles. Myoblast migration is preceded by the formation of <a href="/wiki/Connective_tissue" title="Connective tissue">connective tissue</a> frameworks, usually formed from the somatic <a href="/wiki/Lateral_plate_mesoderm" title="Lateral plate mesoderm">lateral plate mesoderm</a>. Myoblasts follow chemical signals to the appropriate locations, where they fuse into elongated multinucleated skeletal muscle cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sweeney_1997_33-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sweeney_1997-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>Between the tenth and the eighteenth weeks of gestation, all muscle cells have fast myosin heavy chains; two myotube types become distinguished in the developing <a href="/wiki/Fetus" title="Fetus">fetus</a> – both expressing fast chains but one expressing fast and slow chains. Between 10 and 40 per cent of the fibers express the slow myosin chain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacIntosh_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-MacIntosh-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>Fiber types are established during embryonic development and are remodelled later in the adult by neural and hormonal influences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schiaffino_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schiaffino-25">[25]</a></sup> The population of satellite cells present underneath the basal lamina is necessary for the postnatal development of muscle cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murach_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Murach-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Function">Function</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skeletal_muscle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Function">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The primary function of muscle is <a href="/wiki/Muscle_contraction" title="Muscle contraction">contraction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SEER_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-SEER-2">[2]</a></sup> Following contraction, skeletal muscle functions as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endocrine" title="Endocrine">endocrine</a> organ by secreting <a href="/wiki/Myokine" title="Myokine">myokines</a> – a wide range of <a href="/wiki/Cytokine" title="Cytokine">cytokines</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Peptide" title="Peptide">peptides</a> that act as signalling molecules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grube_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grube-36">[36]</a></sup> Myokines in turn are believed to mediate the health benefits of <a href="/wiki/Exercise" title="Exercise">exercise</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Myokines are secreted into the bloodstream after muscle contraction. <a href="/wiki/Interleukin_6" title="Interleukin 6">Interleukin 6</a> (IL-6) is the most studied myokine, other muscle contraction-induced myokines include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BDNF" title="BDNF">BDNF</a>, <a href="/wiki/FGF21" title="FGF21">FGF21</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secreted_Protein,_Acidic_and_Rich_in_Cysteine" title="Secreted Protein, Acidic and Rich in Cysteine">SPARC</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lee_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lee-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>Muscle also functions to produce body heat. Muscle contraction is responsible for producing 85% of the body's heat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SEER2_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-SEER2-39">[39]</a></sup> This heat produced is as a by-product of muscular activity, and is mostly wasted. As a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homeostatic" title="Homeostatic">homeostatic</a> response to extreme cold, muscles are signaled to trigger contractions of <a href="/wiki/Shivering" title="Shivering">shivering</a> in order to generate heat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stax_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-stax-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contraction">Contraction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skeletal_muscle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Contraction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Muscle_contraction" title="Muscle contraction">Muscle contraction</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1006_Sliding_Filament_Model_of_Muscle_Contraction.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="643" data-file-width="818" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/1006_Sliding_Filament_Model_of_Muscle_Contraction.jpg/220px-1006_Sliding_Filament_Model_of_Muscle_Contraction.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/1006_Sliding_Filament_Model_of_Muscle_Contraction.jpg/330px-1006_Sliding_Filament_Model_of_Muscle_Contraction.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/1006_Sliding_Filament_Model_of_Muscle_Contraction.jpg/440px-1006_Sliding_Filament_Model_of_Muscle_Contraction.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1006_Sliding_Filament_Model_of_Muscle_Contraction.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>When a sarcomere contracts, the Z lines move closer together, and the I band becomes smaller. The A band stays the same width. At full contraction, the thin and thick filaments overlap.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1008_Skeletal_Muscle_Contraction.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1259" data-file-width="811" decoding="async" height="342" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/1008_Skeletal_Muscle_Contraction.jpg/220px-1008_Skeletal_Muscle_Contraction.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/1008_Skeletal_Muscle_Contraction.jpg/330px-1008_Skeletal_Muscle_Contraction.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/1008_Skeletal_Muscle_Contraction.jpg/440px-1008_Skeletal_Muscle_Contraction.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1008_Skeletal_Muscle_Contraction.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Contraction in more detail</div></div></div>
<p>Contraction is achieved by the muscle's structural unit, the muscle fiber, and by its functional unit, the <a href="/wiki/Motor_unit" title="Motor unit">motor unit</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore1_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-Moore1-3">[3]</a></sup> Muscle fibers are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Excitable_cell" title="Excitable cell">excitable cells</a> stimulated by <a href="/wiki/Motor_neuron" title="Motor neuron">motor neurons</a>. The motor unit consists of a motor neuron and the many fibers that it makes contact with. A single muscle is stimulated by many motor units. Muscle fibers are
subject to <a href="/wiki/Depolarization" title="Depolarization">depolarization</a> by the neurotransmitter <a href="/wiki/Acetylcholine" title="Acetylcholine">acetylcholine</a>, released by the motor neurons at the <a href="/wiki/Neuromuscular_junction" title="Neuromuscular junction">neuromuscular junctions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to the <a href="/wiki/Actin" title="Actin">actin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Myosin" title="Myosin">myosin</a> <a href="/wiki/Myofilament" title="Myofilament">myofilaments</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Myofibril" title="Myofibril">myofibrils</a> that make up the contractile <a href="/wiki/Sarcomere" title="Sarcomere">sarcomeres</a>, there are two other important regulatory proteins – <a href="/wiki/Troponin" title="Troponin">troponin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tropomyosin" title="Tropomyosin">tropomyosin</a>, that make muscle contraction possible. These proteins are associated with actin and cooperate to prevent its interaction with myosin. Once a cell is sufficiently stimulated, the cell's <a href="/wiki/Sarcoplasmic_reticulum" title="Sarcoplasmic reticulum">sarcoplasmic reticulum</a> <a href="/wiki/Calcium_signaling" title="Calcium signaling">releases ionic calcium</a> (Ca<sup>2+</sup>), which then interacts with the regulatory protein troponin. Calcium-bound troponin undergoes a conformational change that leads to the movement of tropomyosin, subsequently exposing the myosin-binding sites on actin. This allows for myosin and actin ATP-dependent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cross-bridge_cycle" title="Cross-bridge cycle">cross-bridge cycling</a> and shortening of the muscle.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Excitation-contraction_coupling">Excitation-contraction coupling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skeletal_muscle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Excitation-contraction coupling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Excitation_contraction_coupling" title="Excitation contraction coupling">Excitation contraction coupling</a> is the process by which a <a href="/wiki/Action_potential#Muscular_action_potentials" title="Action potential">muscular action potential</a> in the muscle fiber causes the <a href="/wiki/Myofibril" title="Myofibril">myofibrils</a> to contract. This process relies on a direct coupling between the <a href="/wiki/Sarcoplasmic_reticulum" title="Sarcoplasmic reticulum">sarcoplasmic reticulum</a> calcium release channel <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RYR1" title="RYR1">RYR1</a> (ryanodine receptor 1), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voltage-dependent_calcium_channel" title="Voltage-dependent calcium channel">voltage-gated L-type calcium channels</a> (identified as dihydropyridine receptors, DHPRs). DHPRs are located on the sarcolemma (which includes the surface sarcolemma and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transverse_tubules" title="Transverse tubules">transverse tubules</a>), while the RyRs reside across the SR membrane. The close apposition of a transverse tubule and two SR regions containing RyRs is described as a triad and is predominantly where excitation–contraction coupling takes place. Excitation–contraction coupling occurs when depolarization of skeletal muscle cell results in a muscle action potential, which spreads across the cell surface and into the muscle fiber's network of <a href="/wiki/T-tubule" title="T-tubule">T-tubules</a>, thereby depolarizing the inner portion of the muscle fiber. Depolarization of the inner portions activates dihydropyridine receptors in the terminal cisternae, which are in close proximity to ryanodine receptors in the adjacent <a href="/wiki/Endoplasmic_reticulum#Sarcoplasmic_reticulum" title="Endoplasmic reticulum">sarcoplasmic reticulum</a>. The activated dihydropyridine receptors physically interact with ryanodine receptors to activate them via foot processes (involving conformational changes that allosterically activates the ryanodine receptors). As the ryanodine receptors open, <span class="chemf nowrap">Ca<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> is released from the sarcoplasmic reticulum into the local junctional space and diffuses into the bulk cytoplasm to cause a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calcium_spark" title="Calcium spark">calcium spark</a>. Note that the sarcoplasmic reticulum has a large <a href="/wiki/Calcium_buffering" title="Calcium buffering">calcium buffering</a> capacity partially due to a <a href="/wiki/Calcium-binding_protein" title="Calcium-binding protein">calcium-binding protein</a> called <a href="/wiki/Calsequestrin" title="Calsequestrin">calsequestrin</a>. The near synchronous activation of thousands of <a href="/wiki/Calcium_sparks" title="Calcium sparks">calcium sparks</a> by the action potential causes a cell-wide increase in calcium giving rise to the upstroke of the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Calcium_transient&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Calcium transient (page does not exist)">calcium transient</a>. The <span class="chemf nowrap">Ca<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> released into the cytosol binds to <a href="/wiki/Troponin_C" title="Troponin C">Troponin C</a> by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Actin_filaments" title="Actin filaments">actin filaments</a>, to allow crossbridge cycling, producing force and, in some situations, motion. The <a href="/wiki/SERCA" title="SERCA">sarco/endoplasmic reticulum calcium-ATPase</a> (SERCA) actively pumps <span class="chemf nowrap">Ca<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> back into the sarcoplasmic reticulum. As <span class="chemf nowrap">Ca<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> declines back to resting levels, the force declines and relaxation occurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Muscle_movement">Muscle movement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skeletal_muscle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Muscle movement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .ambox-move{border-left:10px solid #9932cc}.mw-parser-output .ambox-protection{border-left:10px solid #a2a9b1}.mw-parser-output .ambox .mbox-text{border:none;padding:0.25em .mbox-text-span{margin-left:23px!important}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .ambox{margin:0 10%}}</style><table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x,
