<b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Skeletal_muscle&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">June 2021</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Efferent_nerve_fiber" title="Efferent nerve fiber">efferent</a> leg of the <a href="/wiki/Peripheral_nervous_system" title="Peripheral nervous system">peripheral nervous system</a> is responsible for conveying commands to the muscles and glands, and is ultimately responsible for voluntary movement.  <a href="/wiki/Nerve" title="Nerve">Nerves</a> move muscles in response to <a href="/wiki/Somatic_nervous_system" title="Somatic nervous system">voluntary</a> and <a href="/wiki/Autonomic_nervous_system" title="Autonomic nervous system">autonomic</a> (involuntary) signals from the <a href="/wiki/Brain" title="Brain">brain</a>. Deep muscles, superficial muscles, <a href="/wiki/Template:Muscles_of_head" title="Template:Muscles of head">muscles of the face</a> and internal muscles all correspond with dedicated regions in the primary motor cortex of the <a href="/wiki/Human_brain" title="Human brain">brain</a>, directly anterior to the central sulcus that divides the frontal and parietal lobes.
</p><p>In addition, muscles react to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reflex_action" title="Reflex action">reflexive</a> nerve stimuli that do not always send signals all the way to the brain. In this case, the signal from the afferent fiber does not reach the brain, but produces the reflexive movement by direct connections with the efferent nerves in the <a href="/wiki/Spinal_cord" title="Spinal cord">spine</a>. However, the majority of muscle activity is volitional, and the result of complex interactions between various areas of the brain.
</p><p>Nerves that control skeletal muscles in <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a> correspond with neuron groups along the <a href="/wiki/Primary_motor_cortex" title="Primary motor cortex">primary motor cortex</a> of the brain's <a href="/wiki/Cerebral_cortex" title="Cerebral cortex">cerebral cortex</a>. Commands are routed through the <a href="/wiki/Basal_ganglia" title="Basal ganglia">basal ganglia</a> and are modified by input from the <a href="/wiki/Cerebellum" title="Cerebellum">cerebellum</a> before being relayed through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pyramidal_tract" title="Pyramidal tract">pyramidal tract</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Spinal_cord" title="Spinal cord">spinal cord</a> and from there to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Motor_end_plate" title="Motor end plate">motor end plate</a> at the muscles. Along the way, feedback, such as that of the <a href="/wiki/Extrapyramidal_system" title="Extrapyramidal system">extrapyramidal system</a> contribute signals to influence <a href="/wiki/Muscle_tone" title="Muscle tone">muscle tone</a> and response.
</p><p>Deeper muscles such as those involved in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_position" title="Human position">posture</a> often are controlled from nuclei in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brain_stem" title="Brain stem">brain stem</a> and basal ganglia.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Proprioception">Proprioception</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skeletal_muscle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Proprioception">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Proprioception" title="Proprioception">Proprioception</a></div>
<p>In skeletal muscles, <a href="/wiki/Muscle_spindle" title="Muscle spindle">muscle spindles</a> convey information about the degree of muscle length and stretch to the central nervous system to assist in maintaining posture and joint position. The <a href="/wiki/Sense" title="Sense">sense</a> of where our bodies are in space is called <a href="/wiki/Proprioception" title="Proprioception">proprioception</a>, the perception of body awareness, the "unconscious" awareness of where the various regions of the body are located at any one time. Several areas in the brain coordinate movement and position with the feedback information gained from proprioception. The cerebellum and <a href="/wiki/Red_nucleus" title="Red nucleus">red nucleus</a> in particular continuously sample position against movement and make minor corrections to assure smooth motion.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_consumption">Energy consumption</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skeletal_muscle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Energy consumption">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bioenergetic_systems" title="Bioenergetic systems">Bioenergetic systems</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1016_Muscle_Metabolism.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1014" data-file-width="902" decoding="async" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/1016_Muscle_Metabolism.jpg/220px-1016_Muscle_Metabolism.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/1016_Muscle_Metabolism.jpg/330px-1016_Muscle_Metabolism.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/1016_Muscle_Metabolism.jpg/440px-1016_Muscle_Metabolism.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1016_Muscle_Metabolism.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>(a) Some ATP is stored in a resting muscle. As contraction starts, it is used up in seconds. More ATP is generated from creatine phosphate for about 15 seconds. (b) Each glucose molecule produces two ATP and two molecules of pyruvic acid, which can be used in aerobic respiration or converted to <a href="/wiki/Lactic_acid" title="Lactic acid">lactic acid</a>. If oxygen is not available, pyruvic acid is converted to lactic acid, which may contribute to <a href="/wiki/Muscle_fatigue" title="Muscle fatigue">muscle fatigue</a>. This occurs during strenuous exercise when high amounts of energy are needed but oxygen cannot be sufficiently delivered to muscle. (c) Aerobic respiration is the breakdown of glucose in the presence of oxygen (O2) to produce carbon dioxide, water, and ATP. Approximately 95 percent of the ATP required for resting or moderately active muscles is provided by aerobic respiration, which takes place in mitochondria.</div></div></div>
<p>Muscular activity accounts for much of the body's <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> consumption. All muscle cells produce <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">adenosine triphosphate</a> (ATP) molecules which are used to power the movement of the <a href="/wiki/Myosin_head" title="Myosin head">myosin heads</a>. Muscles have a short-term store of energy in the form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Creatine_phosphate" title="Creatine phosphate">creatine phosphate</a> which is generated from ATP and can regenerate ATP when needed with <a href="/wiki/Creatine_kinase" title="Creatine kinase">creatine kinase</a>. Muscles also keep a storage form of glucose in the form of <a href="/wiki/Glycogen" title="Glycogen">glycogen</a>. Glycogen can be rapidly converted to <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a> when energy is required for sustained, powerful contractions. Within the voluntary skeletal muscles, the glucose molecule can be metabolized anaerobically in a process called glycolysis which produces two ATP and two <a href="/wiki/Lactic_acid" title="Lactic acid">lactic acid</a> molecules in the process (note that in aerobic conditions, lactate is not formed; instead <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pyruvate" title="Pyruvate">pyruvate</a> is formed and transmitted through the <a href="/wiki/Citric_acid_cycle" title="Citric acid cycle">citric acid cycle</a>). Muscle cells also contain globules of fat, which are used for energy during <a href="/wiki/Aerobic_exercise" title="Aerobic exercise">aerobic exercise</a>. The aerobic energy systems take longer to produce the ATP and reach peak efficiency, and requires many more biochemical steps, but produces significantly more ATP than anaerobic glycolysis. Cardiac muscle on the other hand, can readily consume any of the three macronutrients (protein, glucose and fat) aerobically without a 'warm up' period and always extracts the maximum ATP yield from any molecule involved. The heart, liver and red blood cells will also consume lactic acid produced and excreted by skeletal muscles during exercise.
</p><p>Skeletal muscle uses more calories than other organs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heydermann_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heydermann-43">[43]</a></sup>
At rest it consumes 54.4 kJ/kg (13.0 kcal/kg) per day. This is larger than <a href="/wiki/Adipose_tissue" title="Adipose tissue">adipose tissue</a> (fat) at 18.8 kJ/kg (4.5 kcal/kg), and bone at 9.6 kJ/kg (2.3 kcal/kg).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heymsfield_2002_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heymsfield_2002-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Efficiency">Efficiency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skeletal_muscle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Efficiency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Skeletal_muscle&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">June 2021</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_efficiency" title="Mechanical efficiency">efficiency</a> of human muscle has been measured (in the context of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Watercraft_rowing" title="Watercraft rowing">rowing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cycling" title="Cycling">cycling</a>) at 18% to 26%. The efficiency is defined as the ratio of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mechanical_work" title="Mechanical work">mechanical work</a> output to the total <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolic</a> cost, as can be calculated from oxygen consumption. This low efficiency is the result of about 40% efficiency of generating <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">ATP</a> from <a href="/wiki/Food_energy" title="Food energy">food energy</a>, losses in converting energy from ATP into mechanical work inside the muscle, and mechanical losses inside the body. The latter two losses are dependent on the type of exercise and the type of muscle fibers being used (fast-twitch or slow-twitch). For an overall efficiency of 20 percent, one watt of mechanical power is equivalent to 4.3 kcal per hour. For example, one manufacturer of rowing equipment calibrates its <a href="/wiki/Indoor_rower" title="Indoor rower">rowing ergometer</a> to count burned calories as equal to four times the actual mechanical work, plus 300 kcal per hour, this amounts to about 20 percent efficiency at 250 watts of mechanical output.  The mechanical energy output of a cyclic contraction can depend upon many factors, including activation timing, muscle strain trajectory, and rates of force rise &amp; decay.  These can be synthesized experimentally using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Work_Loop" title="Work Loop">work loop analysis</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Muscle_strength">Muscle strength</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skeletal_muscle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Muscle strength">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Physical_strength" title="Physical strength">Physical strength</a></div>
<p>Muscle strength is a result of three overlapping factors: <i>physiological strength</i> (muscle size, cross sectional area, available crossbridging, responses to training), <i>neurological strength</i> (how strong or weak is the signal that tells the muscle to contract), and <i>mechanical strength</i> (muscle's force angle on the lever, moment arm length, joint capabilities).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="float:right; margin:10px;">
<caption>Grading of muscle strength
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<td>Grade 0</td>
<td>No contraction
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Grade 1</td>
<td>Trace of contraction, but no movement at the joint
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Grade 2</td>
<td>Movement at the joint with gravity eliminated
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Grade 3</td>
<td>Movement against gravity, but not against added resistance
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Grade 4</td>
<td>Movement against external resistance, but less than normal
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Grade 5</td>
<td>Normal strength
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Vertebrate muscle typically produces approximately 25–33 <a href="/wiki/Newton_(unit)" title="Newton (unit)">N</a> (5.6–7.4 <a href="/wiki/Pound_(force)" title="Pound (force)">lb<sub>f</sub></a>) of force per square centimeter of muscle cross-sectional area when isometric and at optimal length.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Some <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrate</a> muscles, such as in crab claws, have much longer <a href="/wiki/Sarcomere" title="Sarcomere">sarcomeres</a> than vertebrates, resulting in many more sites for actin and myosin to bind and thus much greater force per square centimeter at the cost of much slower speed.  The force generated by a contraction can be measured non-invasively using either <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mechanomyography" title="Mechanomyography">mechanomyography</a> or <a href="/wiki/Phonomyography" title="Phonomyography">phonomyography</a>, be measured <a href="/wiki/In_vivo" title="In vivo">in vivo</a> using tendon strain (if a prominent tendon is present), or be measured directly using more invasive methods.
</p><p>The strength of any given muscle, in terms of force exerted on the skeleton, depends upon <a href="/wiki/Muscle_contraction#Force-length_and_force-velocity_relationships" title="Muscle contraction">length, shortening speed</a>, cross sectional area, <a href="/wiki/Pennate_muscle" title="Pennate muscle">pennation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sarcomere" title="Sarcomere">sarcomere</a> length, <a href="/wiki/Myosin" title="Myosin">myosin</a> isoforms, and neural activation of <a href="/wiki/Motor_unit" title="Motor unit">motor units</a>.  Significant reductions in muscle strength can indicate underlying pathology, with the chart at right used as a guide.
</p><p>The <i>maximum holding time</i> for a contracted muscle depends on its supply of energy and is stated by <a href="/wiki/Rohmert%27s_law" title="Rohmert's law">Rohmert's law</a> to <a href="/wiki/Exponential_decay" title="Exponential decay">exponentially decay</a> from the beginning of exertion.
</p>
<h4><span id="The_.22strongest.22_human_muscle"></span><span class="mw-headline" id='The_"strongest"_human_muscle'>The "strongest" human muscle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skeletal_muscle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title='Edit section: The "strongest" human muscle'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-More_citations_needed_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Skeletal_muscle&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">March 2016</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Since three factors affect muscular strength simultaneously and muscles never work individually, it is misleading to compare strength in individual muscles, and state that one is the "strongest". But below are several muscles whose strength is noteworthy for different reasons.
</p>
<ul><li>In ordinary parlance, muscular "strength" usually refers to the ability to exert a force on an external object—for example, lifting a weight. By this definition, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Masseter" title="Masseter">masseter</a> or <a href="/wiki/Jaw" title="Jaw">jaw</a> muscle is the strongest. The 1992 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guinness_Book_of_Records" title="Guinness Book of Records">Guinness Book of Records</a> records the achievement of a bite strength of 4,337 <a href="/wiki/Newton_(unit)" title="Newton (unit)">N</a> (975 <a href="/wiki/Pound_(force)" title="Pound (force)">lb<sub>f</sub></a>) for 2 seconds. What distinguishes the masseter is not anything special about the muscle itself, but its advantage in working against a much shorter lever arm than other muscles.</li>
<li>If "strength" refers to the force exerted by the muscle itself, e.g., on the place where it inserts into a bone, then the strongest muscles are those with the largest cross-sectional area. This is because the tension exerted by an individual skeletal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muscle_fiber" title="Muscle fiber">muscle fiber</a> does not vary much. Each fiber can exert a force on the order of 0.3 micronewton. By this definition, the strongest muscle of the body is usually said to be the <a href="/wiki/Quadriceps" title="Quadriceps">quadriceps femoris</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Gluteus_maximus" title="Gluteus maximus">gluteus maximus</a>.</li>
<li>Because muscle strength is determined by cross-sectional area, a shorter muscle will be stronger "pound for pound" (i.e., by <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">weight</a>) than a longer muscle of the same cross-sectional area. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Myometrial" title="Myometrial">myometrial</a> layer of the uterus may be the strongest muscle by weight in the female body. At the time when an <a href="/wiki/Infant" title="Infant">infant</a> is delivered, the entire uterus weighs about 1.1 kg (40 oz). During childbirth, the uterus exerts 100 to 400 N (25 to 100 lbf) of downward force with each contraction.</li>
<li>The external muscles of the eye are conspicuously large and strong in relation to the small size and weight of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_eyeball" title="Human eyeball">eyeball</a>. It is frequently said that they are "the strongest muscles for the job they have to do" and are sometimes claimed to be "100 times stronger than they need to be." However, eye movements (particularly <a href="/wiki/Saccade" title="Saccade">saccades</a> used on facial scanning and reading) do require high speed movements, and eye muscles are exercised nightly during <a href="/wiki/Rapid_eye_movement_sleep" title="Rapid eye movement sleep">rapid eye movement sleep</a>.</li>
<li>The statement that "the <a href="/wiki/Tongue" title="Tongue">tongue</a> is the strongest muscle in the body" appears frequently in lists of surprising facts, but it is difficult to find any definition of "strength" that would make this statement true. Note that the tongue consists of eight muscles, not one.</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Force_generation">Force generation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skeletal_muscle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Force generation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Muscle_architecture#Force_generation" title="Muscle architecture">Muscle force</a> is proportional to <a href="/wiki/Physiological_cross-sectional_area" title="Physiological cross-sectional area">physiological cross-sectional area</a> (PCSA), and muscle velocity is proportional to muscle fiber length.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> The torque around a joint, however, is determined by a number of biomechanical parameters, including the distance between muscle insertions and pivot points, muscle size and <a href="/wiki/Architectural_gear_ratio" title="Architectural gear ratio">architectural gear ratio</a>. Muscles are normally arranged in opposition so that when one group of muscles contracts, another group relaxes or lengthens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-openstax_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-openstax-47">[47]</a></sup> Antagonism in the transmission of nerve impulses to the muscles means that it is impossible to fully stimulate the contraction of two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antagonist_(muscle)" title="Antagonist (muscle)">antagonistic muscles</a> at any one time. During ballistic motions such as throwing, the antagonist muscles act to 'brake' the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agonist_(muscle)" title="Agonist (muscle)">agonist muscles</a> throughout the contraction, particularly at the end of the motion. In the example of throwing, the chest and front of the shoulder (anterior deltoid) contract to pull the arm forward, while the muscles in the back and rear of the shoulder (posterior deltoid) also contract and undergo eccentric contraction to slow the motion down to avoid injury. Part of the training process is learning to relax the antagonist muscles to increase the force input of the chest and anterior shoulder.
</p><p>Contracting muscles produce vibration and sound.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Slow twitch fibers produce 10 to 30 contractions per second (10 to 30 Hz). Fast twitch fibers produce 30 to 70 contractions per second (30 to 70 Hz).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>  The vibration can be witnessed and felt by highly tensing one's muscles, as when making a firm fist. The sound can be heard by pressing a highly tensed muscle against the ear, again a firm fist is a good example. The sound is usually described as a rumbling sound. Some individuals can voluntarily produce this rumbling sound by contracting the <a href="/wiki/Tensor_tympani_muscle" title="Tensor tympani muscle">tensor tympani muscle</a> of the middle ear. The rumbling sound can also be heard when the neck or jaw muscles are highly tensed.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Signal_transduction_pathways">Signal transduction pathways</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skeletal_muscle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Signal transduction pathways">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Skeletal_muscle&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">May 2021</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Skeletal muscle fiber-type phenotype in adult animals is regulated by several independent signaling pathways. These include pathways involved with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ras_subfamily" title="Ras subfamily">Ras</a>/mitogen-activated protein kinase (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MAPK" title="MAPK">MAPK</a>) pathway, calcineurin, calcium/calmodulin-dependent protein kinase IV, and the peroxisome proliferator γ coactivator 1 (PGC-1). The <a href="/wiki/MAPK/ERK_pathway" title="MAPK/ERK pathway">Ras/MAPK signaling pathway</a> links the motor neurons and signaling systems, coupling excitation and transcription regulation to promote the nerve-dependent induction of the slow program in regenerating muscle. <a href="/wiki/Calcineurin" title="Calcineurin">Calcineurin</a>, a Ca<sup>2+</sup>/<a href="/wiki/Calmodulin" title="Calmodulin">calmodulin</a>-activated <a href="/wiki/Phosphatase" title="Phosphatase">phosphatase</a> implicated in nerve activity-dependent fiber-type specification in skeletal muscle, directly controls the phosphorylation state of the transcription factor <a href="/wiki/NFAT" title="NFAT">NFAT</a>, allowing for its translocation to the nucleus and leading to the activation of slow-type muscle proteins in cooperation with myocyte enhancer factor 2 (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MEF2" title="MEF2">MEF2</a>) proteins and other regulatory proteins. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ca2%2B/calmodulin-dependent_protein_kinase" title="Ca2+/calmodulin-dependent protein kinase">Ca2+/calmodulin-dependent protein kinase</a> activity is also upregulated by slow motor neuron activity, possibly because it amplifies the slow-type calcineurin-generated responses by promoting MEF2 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transactivator" title="Transactivator">transactivator</a> functions and enhancing oxidative capacity through stimulation of <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_biogenesis" title="Mitochondrial biogenesis">mitochondrial biogenesis</a>.
</p><p>Contraction-induced changes in intracellular calcium or reactive oxygen species provide signals to diverse pathways that include the MAPKs, calcineurin and calcium/calmodulin-dependent protein kinase IV to activate transcription factors that regulate gene expression and enzyme activity in skeletal muscle.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Muscle_pathways.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1343" data-file-width="2711" decoding="async" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Muscle_pathways.svg/400px-Muscle_pathways.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Muscle_pathways.svg/600px-Muscle_pathways.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Muscle_pathways.svg/800px-Muscle_pathways.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Muscle_pathways.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Exercise-induced signaling pathways in skeletal muscle that determine specialized characteristics of slow- and fast-twitch muscle fibers</div></div></div>
<p>PGC1-α (<a href="/wiki/PPARGC1A" title="PPARGC1A">PPARGC1A</a>), a transcriptional coactivator of nuclear receptors important to the regulation of a number of mitochondrial genes involved in oxidative metabolism, directly interacts with MEF2 to synergistically activate selective slow twitch (ST) muscle genes and also serves as a target for calcineurin signaling. A peroxisome proliferator-activated receptor δ (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PPAR%CE%B4" title="PPARδ">PPARδ</a>)-mediated transcriptional pathway is involved in the regulation of the skeletal muscle fiber phenotype. Mice that harbor an activated form of PPARδ display an "endurance" phenotype, with a coordinated increase in oxidative enzymes and <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_biogenesis" title="Mitochondrial biogenesis">mitochondrial biogenesis</a> and an increased proportion of ST fibers. Thus—through functional genomics—calcineurin, calmodulin-dependent kinase, PGC-1α, and activated PPARδ form the basis of a signaling network that controls skeletal muscle fiber-type transformation and metabolic profiles that protect against insulin resistance and obesity.
</p><p>The transition from aerobic to anaerobic metabolism during intense work requires that several systems are rapidly activated to ensure a constant supply of ATP for the working muscles. These include a switch from fat-based to carbohydrate-based fuels, a redistribution of blood flow from nonworking to exercising muscles, and the removal of several of the by-products of anaerobic metabolism, such as carbon dioxide and lactic acid. Some of these responses are governed by transcriptional control of the fast twitch (FT) glycolytic phenotype. For example, skeletal muscle reprogramming from an ST glycolytic phenotype to an FT glycolytic phenotype involves the Six1/Eya1 complex, composed of members of the Six protein family. Moreover, the hypoxia-inducible factor 1-α (<a href="/wiki/HIF1A" title="HIF1A">HIF1A</a>) has been identified as a master regulator for the expression of genes involved in essential hypoxic responses that maintain ATP levels in cells. <a href="/wiki/Ablation" title="Ablation">Ablation</a> of HIF-1α in skeletal muscle was associated with an increase in the activity of rate-limiting enzymes of the mitochondria, indicating that the citric acid cycle and increased fatty acid oxidation may be compensating for decreased flow through the glycolytic pathway in these animals. However, hypoxia-mediated HIF-1α responses are also linked to the regulation of mitochondrial dysfunction through the formation of excessive reactive oxygen species in mitochondria.
</p><p>Other pathways also influence adult muscle character.  For example, physical force inside a muscle fiber may release the transcription factor <a href="/wiki/Serum_response_factor" title="Serum response factor">serum response factor</a> from the structural protein titin, leading to altered muscle growth.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Exercise">Exercise</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skeletal_muscle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Exercise">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Exercise" title="Exercise">Exercise</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jogging_couple.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2440" data-file-width="3660" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Jogging_couple.jpg/220px-Jogging_couple.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Jogging_couple.jpg/330px-Jogging_couple.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Jogging_couple.jpg/440px-Jogging_couple.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jogging_couple.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Jogging" title="Jogging">Jogging</a> is one form of aerobic exercise.</div></div></div>
<p>Physical exercise is often recommended as a means of improving <a href="/wiki/Motor_skill" title="Motor skill">motor skills</a>, <a href="/wiki/Physical_fitness" title="Physical fitness">fitness</a>, muscle and bone strength, and joint function. Exercise has several effects upon muscles, <a href="/wiki/Connective_tissue" title="Connective tissue">connective tissue</a>, bone, and the nerves that stimulate the muscles.  One such effect is <a href="/wiki/Muscle_hypertrophy" title="Muscle hypertrophy">muscle hypertrophy</a>, an increase in size of muscle due to an increase in the number of muscle fibers or cross-sectional area of myofibrils.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid3698999_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid3698999-50">[50]</a></sup> Muscle changes depend on the type of exercise used.
</p><p>Generally, there are two types of exercise regimes, aerobic and anaerobic. <a href="/wiki/Aerobic_exercise" title="Aerobic exercise">Aerobic exercise</a> (e.g. marathons) involves activities of low intensity but long duration, during which the muscles used are below their maximal contraction strength. Aerobic activities rely on aerobic respiration (i.e. citric acid cycle and electron transport chain) for metabolic energy by consuming fat, protein, carbohydrates, and oxygen. Muscles involved in aerobic exercises contain a higher percentage of Type I (or slow-twitch) muscle fibers, which primarily contain mitochondrial and oxidation enzymes associated with aerobic respiration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid144412_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid144412-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid4403464_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid4403464-52">[52]</a></sup> On the contrary, <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_exercise" title="Anaerobic exercise">anaerobic exercise</a> is associated with activities of high intensity but short duration, such as sprinting or <a href="/wiki/Weight_training" title="Weight training">weight lifting</a>. The anaerobic activities predominately use Type II, fast-twitch, muscle fibers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid6682735_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid6682735-53">[53]</a></sup> Type II muscle fibers rely on glucogenesis for energy during anaerobic exercise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid635587_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid635587-54">[54]</a></sup> During anaerobic exercise, type II fibers consume little oxygen, protein and fat, produce large amounts of lactic acid and are fatigable. Many exercises are partially aerobic and anaerobic; for example, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soccer" title="Soccer">soccer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rock_climbing" title="Rock climbing">rock climbing</a>.
</p><p>The presence of <a href="/wiki/Lactic_acid" title="Lactic acid">lactic acid</a> has an inhibitory effect on ATP generation within the muscle. It can even stop ATP production if the intracellular concentration becomes too high. However, endurance training mitigates the buildup of lactic acid through increased capillarization and myoglobin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid6036801_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid6036801-55">[55]</a></sup> This increases the ability to remove waste products, like lactic acid, out of the muscles in order to not impair muscle function. Once moved out of muscles, lactic acid can be used by other muscles or body tissues as a source of energy, or transported to the liver where it is converted back to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pyruvate" title="Pyruvate">pyruvate</a>. In addition to increasing the level of lactic acid, strenuous exercise results in the loss of potassium ions in muscle. This may facilitate the recovery of muscle function by protecting against fatigue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nielsen_2001_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nielsen_2001-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Delayed_onset_muscle_soreness" title="Delayed onset muscle soreness">Delayed onset muscle soreness</a> is pain or discomfort that may be felt one to three days after exercising and generally subsides two to three days later. Once thought to be caused by lactic acid build-up, a more recent theory is that it is caused by tiny tears in the muscle fibers caused by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eccentric_contraction" title="Eccentric contraction">eccentric contraction</a>, or unaccustomed training levels. Since lactic acid disperses fairly rapidly, it could not explain pain experienced days after exercise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robergs_2004_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Robergs_2004-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Clinical_significance">Clinical significance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skeletal_muscle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Clinical significance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Muscle_disease">Muscle disease</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skeletal_muscle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Muscle disease">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Myopathy" title="Myopathy">Myopathy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neuromuscular_disease" title="Neuromuscular disease">Neuromuscular disease</a></div>
<p>Diseases of skeletal muscle are termed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Myopathies" title="Myopathies">myopathies</a>, while diseases of nerves are called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neuropathies" title="Neuropathies">neuropathies</a>. Both can affect muscle function or cause muscle pain, and fall under the umbrella of <a href="/wiki/Neuromuscular_disease" title="Neuromuscular disease">neuromuscular disease</a>. The cause of many myopathies is attributed to mutations in the various associated muscle proteins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Henderson_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-Henderson-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wang_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wang-58">[58]</a></sup> Some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inflammatory_myopathies" title="Inflammatory myopathies">inflammatory myopathies</a> include <a href="/wiki/Polymyositis" title="Polymyositis">polymyositis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Inclusion_body_myositis" title="Inclusion body myositis">inclusion body myositis</a>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MuscularDystrophy.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="422" data-file-width="839" decoding="async" height="111" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/MuscularDystrophy.png/220px-MuscularDystrophy.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/MuscularDystrophy.png/330px-MuscularDystrophy.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/MuscularDystrophy.png/440px-MuscularDystrophy.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MuscularDystrophy.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In <a href="/wiki/Muscular_dystrophy" title="Muscular dystrophy">muscular dystrophy</a>, the affected tissues become disorganized and the concentration of <a href="/wiki/Dystrophin" title="Dystrophin">dystrophin</a> (green) is greatly reduced.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Neuromuscular_disease" title="Neuromuscular disease">Neuromuscular diseases</a> affect the muscles and their nervous control. In general, problems with nervous control can cause <a href="/wiki/Spasticity" title="Spasticity">spasticity</a> or <a href="/wiki/Paralysis" title="Paralysis">paralysis</a>, depending on the location and nature of the problem. A number of <a href="/wiki/Movement_disorder" title="Movement disorder">movement disorders</a> are caused by <a href="/wiki/Neurological_disorder" title="Neurological disorder">neurological disorders</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Parkinson%27s_disease" title="Parkinson's disease">Parkinson's disease</a> and <a href="/wiki/Huntington%27s_disease" title="Huntington's disease">Huntington's disease</a> where there is central nervous system dysfunction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSD_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-MSD-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>Symptoms of muscle diseases may include <a href="/wiki/Muscle_weakness" title="Muscle weakness">weakness</a>, spasticity, <a href="/wiki/Myoclonus" title="Myoclonus">myoclonus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Myalgia" title="Myalgia">myalgia</a>. Diagnostic procedures that may reveal muscular disorders include testing <a href="/wiki/Creatine_kinase#Laboratory_testing" title="Creatine kinase">creatine kinase levels in the blood</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electromyography" title="Electromyography">electromyography</a> (measuring electrical activity in muscles). In some cases, <a href="/wiki/Muscle_biopsy" title="Muscle biopsy">muscle biopsy</a> may be done to identify a <a href="/wiki/Myopathy" title="Myopathy">myopathy</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Genetic_testing" title="Genetic testing">genetic testing</a> to identify <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> abnormalities associated with specific myopathies and <a href="/wiki/Muscular_dystrophy" title="Muscular dystrophy">dystrophies</a>.
</p><p>A non-invasive <a href="/wiki/Elastography" title="Elastography">elastography</a> technique that measures muscle noise is undergoing experimentation to provide a way of monitoring neuromuscular disease. The sound produced by a muscle comes from the shortening of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Actomyosin" title="Actomyosin">actomyosin</a> <a href="/wiki/Protein_filament" title="Protein filament">filaments</a> along the axis of the muscle. During <a href="/wiki/Muscle_contraction" title="Muscle contraction">contraction</a>, the muscle shortens along its length and expands across its width, producing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vibrations" title="Vibrations">vibrations</a> at the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dumé_2007_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dumé_2007-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hypertrophy">Hypertrophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skeletal_muscle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Hypertrophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Muscle_hypertrophy" title="Muscle hypertrophy">Muscle hypertrophy</a></div>
<p>Independent of strength and performance measures, muscles can be induced to grow larger by a number of factors, including hormone signaling, developmental factors, <a href="/wiki/Strength_training" title="Strength training">strength training</a>, and disease.  Contrary to popular belief, the number of muscle fibres cannot be increased through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physical_exercise" title="Physical exercise">exercise</a>.  Instead, muscles grow larger through a combination of muscle cell growth as new protein filaments are added along with additional mass provided by undifferentiated satellite cells alongside the existing muscle cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poole_1986_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Poole_1986-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>Biological factors such as age and hormone levels can affect muscle hypertrophy. During <a href="/wiki/Puberty" title="Puberty">puberty</a> in males, hypertrophy occurs at an accelerated rate as the levels of growth-stimulating <a href="/wiki/Hormone" title="Hormone">hormones</a> produced by the body increase. Natural hypertrophy normally stops at full growth in the late teens. As <a href="/wiki/Testosterone" title="Testosterone">testosterone</a> is one of the body's major growth hormones, on average, men find hypertrophy much easier to achieve than women. Taking additional testosterone or other <a href="/wiki/Anabolic_steroid" title="Anabolic steroid">anabolic steroids</a> will increase muscular hypertrophy.
</p><p>Muscular, spinal and neural factors all affect muscle building.  Sometimes a person may notice an increase in strength in a given muscle even though only its opposite has been subject to exercise, such as when a bodybuilder finds her left biceps stronger after completing a regimen focusing only on the right biceps.  This phenomenon is called <a href="/wiki/Cross_education" title="Cross education">cross education</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atrophy">Atrophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skeletal_muscle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Atrophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Muscle_atrophy" title="Muscle atrophy">Muscle atrophy</a></div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Sarcopenia" title="Sarcopenia">Sarcopenia</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Japanese_atrocities._Philippines,_China,_Burma,_Japan_-_NARA_-_292600.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="2403" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Japanese_atrocities._Philippines%2C_China%2C_Burma%2C_Japan_-_NARA_-_292600.jpg/180px-Japanese_atrocities._Philippines%2C_China%2C_Burma%2C_Japan_-_NARA_-_292600.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Japanese_atrocities._Philippines%2C_China%2C_Burma%2C_Japan_-_NARA_-_292600.jpg/270px-Japanese_atrocities._Philippines%2C_China%2C_Burma%2C_Japan_-_NARA_-_292600.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Japanese_atrocities._Philippines%2C_China%2C_Burma%2C_Japan_-_NARA_-_292600.jpg/360px-Japanese_atrocities._Philippines%2C_China%2C_Burma%2C_Japan_-_NARA_-_292600.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Japanese_atrocities._Philippines,_China,_Burma,_Japan_-_NARA_-_292600.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Prisoner of war exhibiting muscle loss as a result of <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Every day between one and two percent of muscle is broken down and rebuilt. <a href="/wiki/Physical_inactivity" title="Physical inactivity">Inactivity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a>, <a href="/wiki/Disease" title="Disease">disease</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aging" title="Aging">aging</a> can increase the breakdown leading to <a href="/wiki/Muscle_atrophy" title="Muscle atrophy">muscle atrophy</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sarcopenia" title="Sarcopenia">sarcopenia</a>. Sarcopenia is commonly an age-related process that can cause <a href="/wiki/Frailty_syndrome" title="Frailty syndrome">frailty</a> and its consequences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet2019_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet2019-62">[62]</a></sup>  A decrease in muscle mass may be accompanied by a smaller number and size of the muscle cells as well as lower protein content.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fuster_2007_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fuster_2007-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Spaceflight" title="Spaceflight">Human spaceflight</a>, involving prolonged periods of immobilization and weightlessness is known to result in muscle weakening and atrophy resulting in a loss of as much as 30% of mass in some muscles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roy_1996_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roy_1996-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_MARES_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_MARES-65">[65]</a></sup> Such consequences are also noted in some mammals following <a href="/wiki/Hibernation" title="Hibernation">hibernation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lohuis_2007_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lohuis_2007-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many diseases and conditions including <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancer</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AIDS" title="AIDS">AIDS</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Heart_failure" title="Heart failure">heart failure</a> can cause muscle loss known as <a href="/wiki/Cachexia" title="Cachexia">cachexia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ebner_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ebner-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skeletal_muscle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Myopathies have been modeled with cell culture systems of muscle from healthy or diseased tissue <a href="/wiki/Biopsy" title="Biopsy">biopsies</a>. Another source of skeletal muscle and progenitors is provided by the <a href="/wiki/Directed_differentiation" title="Directed differentiation">directed differentiation</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Induced_pluripotent_stem_cells" title="Induced pluripotent stem cells">pluripotent stem cells</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid26237517_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid26237517-68">[68]</a></sup>
Research on skeletal muscle properties uses many techniques. <a href="/wiki/Electrical_muscle_stimulation" title="Electrical muscle stimulation">Electrical muscle stimulation</a> is used to determine force and contraction speed at different frequencies related to fiber-type composition and mix within an individual muscle group.  <a href="/wiki/In_vitro_muscle_testing" title="In vitro muscle testing">In vitro muscle testing</a> is used for more complete characterization of muscle properties.
</p><p>The electrical activity associated with muscle contraction is measured via <a href="/wiki/Electromyography" title="Electromyography">electromyography</a> (EMG). Skeletal muscle has two physiological responses: relaxation and contraction. The mechanisms for which these responses occur generate electrical activity measured by EMG.  Specifically, EMG can measure the action potential of a skeletal muscle, which occurs from the <a href="/wiki/Hyperpolarization_(biology)" title="Hyperpolarization (biology)">hyperpolarization</a> of the motor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Axons" title="Axons">axons</a> from nerve impulses sent to the muscle.  EMG is used in research for determining if the skeletal muscle of interest is being activated, the amount of <a href="/wiki/Force" title="Force">force</a> generated, and an indicator of <a href="/wiki/Muscle_fatigue" title="Muscle fatigue">muscle fatigue</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid24319999_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid24319999-69">[69]</a></sup> The two types of EMG are intra-muscular EMG and the most common, surface EMG. The EMG signals are much greater when a skeletal muscle is contracting verses relaxing.  However, for smaller and deeper skeletal muscles the EMG signals are reduced and therefore are viewed as a less valued technique for measuring the activation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid23193462_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid23193462-70">[70]</a></sup> In research using EMG, a <a href="/wiki/Muscle_contraction" title="Muscle contraction">maximal voluntary contraction</a> (MVC) is commonly performed on the skeletal muscle of interest, to have reference data for the rest of the EMG recordings during the main experimental testing for that same skeletal muscle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid24622330_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid24622330-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>Research into the development of <a href="/wiki/Artificial_muscle" title="Artificial muscle">artificial muscles</a> includes the use of <a href="/wiki/Electroactive_polymer" title="Electroactive polymer">electroactive polymers</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skeletal_muscle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skeletal_muscle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
